<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Sea</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Large body of salt water</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article focuses on human experience, history and culture of the collective seas of Earth. For natural science aspects, see more at <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">Ocean</a>. For individual seas, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_seas" title="List of seas">List of seas</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sea_(disambiguation)" title="Sea (disambiguation)">Sea (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/The_Sea_(disambiguation)" title="The Sea (disambiguation)">The Sea (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paracas_National_Reserve,_Ica,_Peru-3April2011.jpg"><img alt="Waves breaking on the shore" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1965" data-file-width="2956" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Paracas_National_Reserve%2C_Ica%2C_Peru-3April2011.jpg/300px-Paracas_National_Reserve%2C_Ica%2C_Peru-3April2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Paracas_National_Reserve%2C_Ica%2C_Peru-3April2011.jpg/450px-Paracas_National_Reserve%2C_Ica%2C_Peru-3April2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Paracas_National_Reserve%2C_Ica%2C_Peru-3April2011.jpg/600px-Paracas_National_Reserve%2C_Ica%2C_Peru-3April2011.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paracas_National_Reserve,_Ica,_Peru-3April2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coastal sea waves at <a href="/wiki/Paracas_National_Reserve" title="Paracas National Reserve">Paracas National Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ica,_Peru" title="Ica, Peru">Ica, Peru</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <b>sea</b>, connected as the <b>world ocean</b> or simply the <b><a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a></b>, is the <a href="/wiki/Body_of_water" title="Body of water">body</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">salty water</a> that covers approximately 71 percent of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s surface. The word <i>sea</i> is also used to denote <a href="/wiki/List_of_seas" title="List of seas">second-order sections</a> of the sea, such as the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, as well as certain large, entirely landlocked, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltwater_lake" title="Saltwater lake">saltwater lakes</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>. The sea moderates Earth's <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> and has important roles in the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_cycle" title="Nitrogen cycle">nitrogen cycle</a>. Humans harnessing and studying the sea have been recorded since ancient times, and evidenced well into <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>, while its modern scientific study is called <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a>. The most abundant solid dissolved in <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a> is <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>. The water also contains <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, amongst many other elements, some in minute concentrations. <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">Salinity</a> varies widely, being lower near the surface and the mouths of large rivers and higher in the depths of the ocean; however, the relative proportions of dissolved salts vary little across the oceans.
</p><p>Winds blowing over the surface of the sea produce <a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">waves</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Breaking_wave" title="Breaking wave">break</a> when they enter the shallow water. Winds also create surface currents through friction, setting up slow but stable circulations of water throughout the oceans. The directions of the circulation are governed by several factors, including the shapes of the continents and <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">Earth's rotation</a> (through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>). Deep-sea currents, known as the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">global conveyor belt</a>, carry cold water from near the poles to every ocean. <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">Tides</a>, the generally twice-daily rise and fall of <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea levels</a>, are caused by Earth's rotation and the gravitational effects of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and, to a lesser extent, of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>. Tides may have a very high <a href="/wiki/Tidal_range" title="Tidal range">range</a> in bays or <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a>. <a href="/wiki/Submarine_earthquake" title="Submarine earthquake">Submarine earthquakes</a> arising from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plate</a> movements under the oceans can lead to destructive <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>, as can volcanoes, huge <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a>, or the impact of large <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>.
</p><p>A wide <a href="/wiki/Marine_life" title="Marine life">variety of organisms</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, plants, <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, and animals, lives in the sea, which offers a wide range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_habitat" title="Marine habitat">marine habitats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, ranging vertically from the <a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">sunlit surface</a> and <a href="/wiki/Littoral_zone" title="Littoral zone">shoreline</a> to the great depths and pressures of the cold, dark <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_zone" title="Abyssal zone">abyssal zone</a>, and in latitude from the cold waters under <a href="/wiki/Polar_ice_cap" title="Polar ice cap">polar ice caps</a> to the warm waters of <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical regions</a>. Many of the major groups of organisms evolved in the sea and <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">life may have started</a> there.
</p><p>The sea provides substantial supplies of food for humans, mainly <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, but also <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">mammals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a>, whether caught by fishermen or <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">farmed</a> underwater. Other human uses of the sea include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shipping" title="Shipping">trade</a>, travel, <a href="/wiki/Subsea_(technology)" title="Subsea (technology)">mineral extraction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">power generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare" title="Naval warfare">warfare</a>, and leisure activities such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_swimming" title="Human swimming">swimming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sailing" title="Sailing">sailing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">scuba diving</a>. Many of these activities create <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">marine pollution</a>. The sea has been an integral element for humans throughout history and culture.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_seas" title="List of seas">List of seas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_ocean_map.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/World_ocean_map.gif/280px-World_ocean_map.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/World_ocean_map.gif/420px-World_ocean_map.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/World_ocean_map.gif 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_ocean_map.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animated map exhibiting the world's <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceanic</a> waters. A continuous <a href="/wiki/Body_of_water" title="Body of water">body of water</a> encircling <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, the World Ocean is divided into a number of principal areas with relatively uninhibited interchange among them. Five oceanic divisions are usually defined: <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern</a>; the last two listed are sometimes consolidated into the first three.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oceans_and_seas_boundaries_map-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2029" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Oceans_and_seas_boundaries_map-en.svg/400px-Oceans_and_seas_boundaries_map-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Oceans_and_seas_boundaries_map-en.svg/600px-Oceans_and_seas_boundaries_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Oceans_and_seas_boundaries_map-en.svg/800px-Oceans_and_seas_boundaries_map-en.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oceans_and_seas_boundaries_map-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marginal seas as defined by the <a href="/wiki/International_Maritime_Organization" title="International Maritime Organization">International Maritime Organization</a></div></div></div>
<p>The sea is the interconnected system of all the Earth's oceanic waters, including the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Oceans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> However, the word "sea" can also be used for many specific, much smaller bodies of seawater, such as the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. There is no sharp distinction between seas and <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, though generally seas are smaller, and are often partly (as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginal_sea" title="Marginal sea">marginal seas</a> or particularly as the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_sea_(oceanography)" title="Mediterranean sea (oceanography)">Mediterranean sea</a>) or wholly (as <a href="/wiki/Inland_sea_(geology)" title="Inland sea (geology)">inland seas</a>) enclosed by land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanservice.noaa_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-oceanservice.noaa-2">[2]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Sargasso_Sea" title="Sargasso Sea">Sargasso Sea</a> has no coastline and lies within a circular current, the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Gyre" title="North Atlantic Gyre">North Atlantic Gyre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stow_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 90">: 90 </span></sup> Seas are generally larger than lakes and contain salt water, but the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Galilee" title="Sea of Galilee">Sea of Galilee</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater_lake" title="Freshwater lake">freshwater lake</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[a]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea">United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea</a> states that all of the ocean is "sea".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[b]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_science">Physical science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BlueMarble-2001-2002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="4096" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/BlueMarble-2001-2002.jpg/300px-BlueMarble-2001-2002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/BlueMarble-2001-2002.jpg/450px-BlueMarble-2001-2002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/BlueMarble-2001-2002.jpg/600px-BlueMarble-2001-2002.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BlueMarble-2001-2002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Composite images of the Earth created by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> in 2001</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Physical_oceanography" title="Physical oceanography">Physical oceanography</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> is the only known <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> with seas of liquid <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> on its surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stow_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 22">: 22 </span></sup> although <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> possesses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martian_ice_caps" title="Martian ice caps">ice caps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earth_analog" title="Earth analog">similar planets</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">other solar systems</a> may have oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> Earth's 1,335,000,000 cubic kilometers (320,000,000 cu mi) of sea contain <a href="/wiki/Water_distribution_on_Earth" title="Water distribution on Earth">about 97.2 percent of its known water</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAAcycle_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAAcycle-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[c]</a></sup> and cover approximately 71 percent of its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stow_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 7">: 7 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup> Another 2.15% of Earth's water is frozen, found in the sea ice covering the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>, the ice cap covering <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">adjacent seas</a>, and various <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> and surface deposits around the world. The remainder (about 0.65% of the whole) form <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">underground reservoirs</a> or various stages of the <a href="#the_water_cycle">water cycle</a>, containing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> encountered and used by most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrestrial_life" title="Terrestrial life">terrestrial life</a>: <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">vapor</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">air</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a> it slowly forms, the <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a> falling from them, and the <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a> spontaneously formed as its waters flow again and again to the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAAcycle_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAAcycle-14">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific study</a> of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and Earth's <a href="#Water_cycle">water cycle</a> is <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrology</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrodynamics" title="Hydrodynamics">hydrodynamics</a> studies the <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> of water in motion. The more recent study of the sea in particular is <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a>. This began as the study of the shape of the ocean's <a href="#currents">currents</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> but has since expanded into a large and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multidisciplinary_approach" title="Multidisciplinary approach">multidisciplinary</a> field:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-22">[19]</a></sup> it examines the properties of <a href="#Seawater">seawater</a>; studies <a href="#Waves">waves</a>, <a href="#Tides">tides</a>, and <a href="#Currents">currents</a>; charts <a href="#Coasts">coastlines</a> and maps the <a href="#Basins">seabeds</a>; and studies <a href="#Marine_life">marine life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> The subfield dealing with the sea's motion, its forces, and the forces acting upon it is known as <a href="/wiki/Physical_oceanography" title="Physical oceanography">physical oceanography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">Marine biology</a> (biological oceanography) studies the <a href="#Plants">plants</a>, <a href="#Animals">animals</a>, and other organisms inhabiting <a href="#Habitats">marine ecosystems</a>. Both are informed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_oceanography" title="Chemical oceanography">chemical oceanography</a>, which studies the behavior of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_elements" title="Chemical elements">elements</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecules" title="Molecules">molecules</a> within the oceans: particularly, at the moment, the ocean's role in the <a href="#The_carbon_cycle">carbon cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>'s role in the <a href="#Acidification">increasing acidification</a> of seawater. Marine and maritime <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">geography</a> charts the shape and shaping of the sea, while <a href="/wiki/Marine_geology" title="Marine geology">marine geology</a> (geological oceanography) has provided evidence of <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">continental drift</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composition_of_the_Earth" title="Composition of the Earth">composition</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth" title="Structure of the Earth">structure of the Earth</a>, clarified the process of <a href="/wiki/Sedimentation" title="Sedimentation">sedimentation</a>, and assisted the study of <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">earthquakes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-22">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seawater">Seawater</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Seawater">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">Seawater</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aquarius_spacecraft_first_global_salinity_map_Aug-Sep_2011.jpg"><img alt="Global salinity map" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1156" data-file-width="2340" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Aquarius_spacecraft_first_global_salinity_map_Aug-Sep_2011.jpg/300px-Aquarius_spacecraft_first_global_salinity_map_Aug-Sep_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Aquarius_spacecraft_first_global_salinity_map_Aug-Sep_2011.jpg/450px-Aquarius_spacecraft_first_global_salinity_map_Aug-Sep_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Aquarius_spacecraft_first_global_salinity_map_Aug-Sep_2011.jpg/600px-Aquarius_spacecraft_first_global_salinity_map_Aug-Sep_2011.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aquarius_spacecraft_first_global_salinity_map_Aug-Sep_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Salinity map taken from the Aquarius Spacecraft. The rainbow colours represent salinity levels: red = 40 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%80%B0" title="‰">‰</a>, purple = 30 ‰</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Salinity">Salinity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Salinity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A characteristic of seawater is that it is salty. Salinity is usually measured in parts per thousand (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%80%B0" title="‰">‰</a> or per mil), and the open ocean has about 35 grams (1.2 oz) solids per litre, a salinity of 35 ‰. The Mediterranean Sea is slightly higher at 38 ‰,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup> while the salinity of the northern Red Sea can reach 41‰.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup> In contrast, some landlocked <a href="/wiki/Hypersaline_lake" title="Hypersaline lake">hypersaline lakes</a> have a much higher salinity, for example the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a> has 300 grams (11 oz) dissolved solids per litre (300 ‰).
</p><p>While the constituents of table salt (<a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a>) make up about 85 percent of the solids in solution, there are also other metal ions such as <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, and negative ions including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphate" title="Sulphate">sulphate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bromide" title="Bromide">bromide</a>. Despite variations in the levels of salinity in different seas, the relative composition of the dissolved salts is stable throughout the world's oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millero_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millero-28">[25]</a></sup> Seawater is too saline for humans to drink safely, as the <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a> cannot excrete urine as salty as seawater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right;">

<p>Although the amount of salt in the ocean remains relatively constant within the scale of millions of years, various factors affect the salinity of a body of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-setalleyduauang_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-setalleyduauang-30">[27]</a></sup> Evaporation and by-product of ice formation (known as "brine rejection") increase salinity, whereas <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>, sea ice melt, and runoff from land reduce it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-setalleyduauang_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-setalleyduauang-30">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, for example, has many rivers flowing into it, and thus the sea could be considered as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brackish" title="Brackish">brackish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> is very salty due to its high evaporation rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaaaa_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-noaaaa-32">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature">Temperature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Temperature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sea temperature depends on the amount of solar radiation falling on its surface. In the tropics, with the sun nearly overhead, the temperature of the surface layers can rise to over 30 °C (86 °F) while near the poles the temperature in equilibrium with the sea ice is about −2 °C (28 °F). There is a continuous circulation of water in the oceans. Warm surface currents cool as they move away from the tropics, and the water becomes denser and sinks. The cold water moves back towards the equator as a deep sea current, driven by changes in the temperature and density of the water, before eventually welling up again towards the surface. Deep seawater has a temperature between −2 °C (28 °F) and 5 °C (41 °F) in all parts of the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Seawater with a typical salinity of 35 ‰ has a freezing point of about −1.8 °C (28.8 °F).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> When its temperature becomes low enough, <a href="/wiki/Ice_crystals" title="Ice crystals">ice crystals</a> form on the surface. These break into small pieces and coalesce into flat discs that form a thick suspension known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frazil" title="Frazil">frazil</a>. In calm conditions this freezes into a thin flat sheet known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nilas" title="Nilas">nilas</a>, which thickens as new ice forms on its underside. In more turbulent seas, frazil crystals join into flat discs known as pancakes. These slide under each other and coalesce to form <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">floes</a>. In the process of freezing, salt water and air are trapped between the ice crystals. Nilas may have a salinity of 12–15 ‰, but by the time the <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a> is one year old, this falls to 4–6 ‰.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxygen_concentration">Oxygen concentration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Oxygen concentration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The amount of oxygen found in seawater depends primarily on the plants growing in it. These are mainly algae, including <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>, with some <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Seagrass" title="Seagrass">seagrasses</a>. In daylight the <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic activity</a> of these plants produces oxygen, which dissolves in the seawater and is used by marine animals. At night, photosynthesis stops, and the amount of dissolved oxygen declines. In the deep sea, where insufficient light penetrates for plants to grow, there is very little dissolved oxygen. In its absence, organic material is broken down by <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic</a> bacteria producing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulphide" title="Hydrogen sulphide">hydrogen sulphide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> is likely to reduce levels of oxygen in surface waters, since the solubility of oxygen in water falls at higher temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ocean_deoxygenation" title="Ocean deoxygenation">Ocean deoxygenation</a> is projected to increase <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(environmental)" title="Hypoxia (environmental)">hypoxia</a> by 10%, and triple suboxic waters (oxygen concentrations 98% less than the mean surface concentrations), for each 1 °C of upper ocean warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Light">Light</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Light">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The amount of light that penetrates the sea depends on the angle of the sun, the weather conditions and the <a href="/wiki/Turbidity" title="Turbidity">turbidity</a> of the water. Much light gets reflected at the surface, and red light gets absorbed in the top few metres. Yellow and green light reach greater depths, and blue and violet light may penetrate as deep as 1,000 metres (3,300 ft). There is insufficient light for photosynthesis and plant growth beyond a depth of about 200 metres (660 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_level">Sea level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sea level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">Sea level</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">Sea level rise</a></div>
<p>Over most of geologic time, the sea level has been higher than it is today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stow_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 74">: 74 </span></sup> The main factor affecting sea level over time is the result of changes in the oceanic crust, with a downward trend expected to continue in the very long term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_maximum" title="Last glacial maximum">last glacial maximum</a>, some 20,000 years ago, the sea level was about 125 metres (410 ft) lower than in present times (2012).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cLevel_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-cLevel-40">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>For at least the last 100 years, <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level has been rising</a> at an average rate of about 1.8 millimetres (0.071 in) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup> Most of this rise can be attributed to an increase in the temperature of the sea due to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, and the resulting slight thermal expansion of the upper 500 metres (1,600 ft) of water. Additional contributions, as much as one quarter of the total, come from water sources on land, such as <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">melting snow and glaciers</a> and extraction of groundwater for irrigation and other agricultural and human needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waves">Waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="13" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv" height="165" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv/220px--Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="3319011" data-framerate="25" data-height="292" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file, 390 × 292 (3.32 Mbps)" data-width="390" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv" type='video/ogg; codecs="theora"'/><source data-bandwidth="90776" data-framerate="25" data-height="120" data-shorttitle="VP9 120P" data-title="Lowest bandwidth VP9 (120P)" data-transcodekey="120p.vp9.webm" data-width="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv.120p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="128744" data-framerate="25" data-height="160" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv.160p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="197344" data-framerate="25" data-height="180" data-shorttitle="VP9 180P" data-title="Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)" data-transcodekey="180p.vp9.webm" data-width="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv.180p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="255856" data-framerate="25" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv.240p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="321368" data-framerate="25" data-height="240" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/fa/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv/Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steep_deep_water_wave.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Movement of molecules as waves pass</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif"><img alt="Diagram showing wave approaching shore" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="429" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif/220px-Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif/330px-Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2d/Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Propagation_du_tsunami_en_profondeur_variable.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>When the wave enters shallow water, it slows down and its amplitude (height) increases.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">Wind wave</a></div>
<p>Wind blowing over the surface of a body of water forms <a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">waves</a> that are perpendicular to the direction of the wind. The friction between air and water caused by a gentle breeze on a pond causes <a href="/wiki/Capillary_wave" title="Capillary wave">ripples</a> to form. A strong blow over the ocean causes larger waves as the moving air pushes against the raised ridges of water. The waves reach their maximum height when the rate at which they are travelling nearly matches the speed of the wind. In open water, when the wind blows continuously as happens in the Southern Hemisphere in the <a href="/wiki/Roaring_Forties" title="Roaring Forties">Roaring Forties</a>, long, organised masses of water called <a href="/wiki/Swell_(ocean)" title="Swell (ocean)">swell</a> roll across the ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stow_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-Stow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 83–84">: 83–84 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-43">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[d]</a></sup> If the wind dies down, the wave formation is reduced, but already-formed waves continue to travel in their original direction until they meet land. The size of the waves depends on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fetch_(geography)" title="Fetch (geography)">fetch</a>, the distance that the wind has blown over the water and the strength and duration of that wind. When waves meet others coming from different directions, interference between the two can produce broken, irregular seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-43">[40]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constructive_interference" title="Constructive interference">Constructive interference</a> can cause individual (unexpected) <a href="/wiki/Rogue_wave" title="Rogue wave">rogue waves</a> much higher than normal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mrgarrison_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-mrgarrison-46">[42]</a></sup> Most waves are less than 3 m (10 ft) high<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mrgarrison_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-mrgarrison-46">[42]</a></sup> and it is not unusual for strong storms to double or triple that height;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Offshore_construction" title="Offshore construction">offshore construction</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_farm" title="Offshore wind farm">wind farms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">oil platforms</a> use <a href="/wiki/Metocean" title="Metocean">metocean</a> statistics from measurements in computing the wave forces (due to for instance the <a href="/wiki/Hundred-year_wave" title="Hundred-year wave">hundred-year wave</a>) they are designed against.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup> Rogue waves, however, have been documented at heights above 25 meters (82 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The top of a wave is known as the crest, the lowest point between waves is the trough and the distance between the crests is the wavelength. The wave is pushed across the surface of the sea by the wind, but this represents a transfer of energy and not a horizontal movement of water. As waves approach land and <a href="/wiki/Waves_and_shallow_water" title="Waves and shallow water">move into shallow water</a>, they change their behavior. If approaching at an angle, waves may bend (<a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a>) or wrap rocks and headlands (<a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a>). When the wave reaches a point where its deepest oscillations of the water contact the <a href="/wiki/Seabed" title="Seabed">seabed</a>, they begin to slow down. This pulls the crests closer together and increases the <a href="/wiki/Wave_height" title="Wave height">waves' height</a>, which is called <a href="/wiki/Wave_shoaling" title="Wave shoaling">wave shoaling</a>. When the ratio of the wave's height to the water depth increases above a certain limit, it "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_breaking" title="Wave breaking">breaks</a>", toppling over in a mass of foaming water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mrgarrison_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-mrgarrison-46">[42]</a></sup> This rushes in a sheet up the beach before retreating into the sea under the influence of gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-43">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tsunami">Tsunami</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tsunami">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2004-tsunami.jpg"><img alt="Tsunami in Thailand" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1114" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/2004-tsunami.jpg/220px-2004-tsunami.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/2004-tsunami.jpg/330px-2004-tsunami.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/2004-tsunami.jpg/440px-2004-tsunami.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2004-tsunami.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/2004_Indian_Ocean_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2004 Indian Ocean earthquake and tsunami">2004 tsunami</a> in Thailand</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">Tsunami</a></div>
<p>A tsunami is an unusual form of wave caused by an infrequent powerful event such as an underwater earthquake or landslide, a meteorite impact, a volcanic eruption or a collapse of land into the sea. These events can temporarily lift or lower the surface of the sea in the affected area, usually by a few feet. The potential energy of the displaced seawater is turned into kinetic energy, creating a shallow wave, a tsunami, radiating outwards at a velocity proportional to the square root of the depth of the water and which therefore travels much faster in the open ocean than on a continental shelf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgstsunami_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgstsunami-51">[47]</a></sup> In the deep open sea, tsunamis have wavelengths of around 80 to 300 miles (130 to 480 km), travel at speeds of over 600 miles per hour (970 km/hr)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NTWC-physics_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NTWC-physics-52">[48]</a></sup> and usually have a height of less than three feet, so they often pass unnoticed at this stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UoW_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-UoW-53">[49]</a></sup> In contrast, ocean surface waves caused by winds have wavelengths of a few hundred feet, travel at up to 65 miles per hour (105 km/h) and are up to 45 feet (14 metres) high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UoW_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-UoW-53">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a tsunami <a href="/wiki/Wave_shoaling" title="Wave shoaling">moves into shallower water</a> its speed decreases, its wavelength shortens and its amplitude increases enormously,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UoW_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-UoW-53">[49]</a></sup> behaving in the same way as a wind-generated wave in shallow water, but on a vastly greater scale. Either the trough or the crest of a tsunami can arrive at the coast first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgstsunami_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-usgstsunami-51">[47]</a></sup> In the former case, the sea draws back and leaves subtidal areas close to the shore exposed which provides a useful warning for people on land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[50]</a></sup> When the crest arrives, it does not usually break but rushes inland, flooding all in its path. Much of the destruction may be caused by the flood water draining back into the sea after the tsunami has struck, dragging debris and people with it. Often several tsunami are caused by a single geological event and arrive at intervals of between eight minutes and two hours. The first wave to arrive on shore may not be the biggest or most destructive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgstsunami_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-usgstsunami-51">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Currents">Currents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Currents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corrientes-oceanicas.png"><img alt="Map showing surface currents" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="522" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Corrientes-oceanicas.png/300px-Corrientes-oceanicas.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Corrientes-oceanicas.png/450px-Corrientes-oceanicas.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Corrientes-oceanicas.png/600px-Corrientes-oceanicas.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corrientes-oceanicas.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Surface currents: red–warm, blue–cold</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">Ocean current</a></div>
<p>Wind blowing over the surface of the sea causes <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> at the interface between air and sea. Not only does this cause waves to form but it also makes the surface seawater move in the same direction as the wind. Although winds are variable, in any one place they predominantly blow from a single direction and thus a surface current can be formed. Westerly winds are most frequent in the mid-latitudes while easterlies dominate the tropics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> When water moves in this way, other water flows in to fill the gap and a circular movement of surface currents known as a <a href="/wiki/Ocean_gyre" title="Ocean gyre">gyre</a> is formed. There are five main gyres in the world's oceans: two in the Pacific, two in the Atlantic and one in the Indian Ocean. Other smaller gyres are found in lesser seas and a single gyre flows around <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. These gyres have followed the same routes for millennia, guided by the <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a> of the land, the wind direction and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>. The surface currents flow in a clockwise direction in the Northern Hemisphere and anticlockwise in the Southern Hemisphere. The water moving away from the equator is warm, and that flowing in the reverse direction has lost most of its heat. These currents tend to moderate the Earth's climate, cooling the equatorial region and warming regions at higher latitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAAcurrent_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAAcurrent-56">[52]</a></sup> Global climate and <a href="/wiki/Weather_forecasting" title="Weather forecasting">weather forecasts</a> are powerfully affected by the world ocean, so <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_model" title="Global climate model">global climate modelling</a> makes use of <a href="/wiki/List_of_ocean_circulation_models" title="List of ocean circulation models">ocean circulation models</a> as well as models of other major components such as the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, land surfaces, aerosols and sea ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup> Ocean models make use of a branch of physics, <a href="/wiki/Geophysical_fluid_dynamics" title="Geophysical fluid dynamics">geophysical fluid dynamics</a>, that describes the large-scale flow of fluids such as seawater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png"><img alt="Map showing the global conveyor belt" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="690" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png/300px-Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png/450px-Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png/600px-Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thermohaline_Circulation_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The global conveyor belt shown in blue with warmer surface currents in red</div></div></div>
<p>Surface currents only affect the top few hundred metres of the sea, but there are also large-scale flows in the ocean depths caused by the movement of deep water masses. A main deep ocean current flows through all the world's oceans and is known as the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> or global conveyor belt. This movement is slow and is driven by differences in density of the water caused by variations in salinity and temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup> At high latitudes the water is chilled by the low atmospheric temperature and becomes saltier as sea ice crystallizes out. Both these factors make it denser, and the water sinks. From the deep sea near Greenland, such water flows southwards between the continental landmasses on either side of the Atlantic. When it reaches the Antarctic, it is joined by further masses of cold, sinking water and flows eastwards. It then splits into two streams that move northwards into the Indian and Pacific Oceans. Here it is gradually warmed, becomes less dense, rises towards the surface and loops back on itself. It takes a thousand years for this circulation pattern to be completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAAcurrent_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAAcurrent-56">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides gyres, there are temporary surface currents that occur under specific conditions. When waves meet a shore at an angle, a <a href="/wiki/Longshore_drift" title="Longshore drift">longshore current</a> is created as water is pushed along parallel to the coastline. The water swirls up onto the beach at right angles to the approaching waves but drains away straight down the slope under the effect of gravity. The larger the breaking waves, the longer the beach and the more oblique the wave approach, the stronger is the longshore current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup> These currents can shift great volumes of sand or pebbles, create <a href="/wiki/Spit_(landform)" title="Spit (landform)">spits</a> and make beaches disappear and water channels silt up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAAcurrent_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-NOAAcurrent-56">[52]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Rip_current" title="Rip current">rip current</a> can occur when water piles up near the shore from advancing waves and is funnelled out to sea through a channel in the seabed. It may occur at a gap in a <a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">sandbar</a> or near a man-made structure such as a <a href="/wiki/Groyne" title="Groyne">groyne</a>. These strong currents can have a velocity of 3 ft (0.9 m) per second, can form at different places at different stages of the tide and can carry away unwary bathers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup> Temporary upwelling currents occur when the wind pushes water away from the land and deeper water rises to replace it. This cold water is often rich in nutrients and creates blooms of phytoplankton and a great increase in the productivity of the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAAcurrent_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-NOAAcurrent-56">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tides">Tides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">Tide</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tides_overview.png"><img alt="Diagram showing how the sun and moon cause tides" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1718" data-file-width="1914" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Tides_overview.png/220px-Tides_overview.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Tides_overview.png/330px-Tides_overview.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Tides_overview.png/440px-Tides_overview.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tides_overview.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>High tides (blue) at the nearest and furthest points of the Earth from the Moon</div></div></div>
<p>Tides are the regular rise and fall in water level experienced by seas and oceans in response to the <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational</a> influences of the Moon and the Sun, and the effects of the Earth's rotation. During each tidal cycle, at any given place the water rises to a maximum height known as "high tide" before ebbing away again to the minimum "low tide" level. As the water recedes, it uncovers more and more of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreshore" title="Foreshore">foreshore</a>, also known as the intertidal zone. The difference in height between the high tide and low tide is known as the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_range" title="Tidal range">tidal range</a> or tidal amplitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanservice_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-oceanservice-62">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most places experience two high tides each day, occurring at intervals of about 12 hours and 25 minutes. This is half the 24 hours and 50 minute period that it takes for the Earth to make a complete revolution and return the Moon to its previous position relative to an observer. The Moon's mass is some 27 million times smaller than the Sun, but it is 400 times closer to the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAAtides_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAAtides-64">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">Tidal force</a> or tide-raising force decreases rapidly with distance, so the moon has more than twice as great an effect on tides as the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAAtides_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAAtides-64">[60]</a></sup> A bulge is formed in the ocean at the place where the Earth is closest to the Moon, because it is also where the effect of the Moon's gravity is stronger. On the opposite side of the Earth, the lunar force is at its weakest and this causes another bulge to form. As the Moon rotates around the Earth, so do these ocean bulges move around the Earth. The gravitational attraction of the Sun is also working on the seas, but its effect on tides is less powerful than that of the Moon, and when the Sun, Moon and Earth are all aligned (full moon and new moon), the combined effect results in the high "spring tides". In contrast, when the Sun is at 90° from the Moon as viewed from Earth, the combined gravitational effect on tides is less causing the lower "neap tides".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanservice_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-oceanservice-62">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surge</a> can occur when high winds pile water up against the coast in a shallow area and this, coupled with a low pressure system, can raise the surface of the sea at high tide dramatically.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_basins">Ocean basins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ocean basins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="429" data-file-width="775" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png/300px-Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png/450px-Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png/600px-Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tectonic_plate_boundaries.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three types of plate boundary</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_basin" title="Ocean basin">Ocean basin</a></div>
<p>The Earth is composed of a magnetic central <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a>, a mostly liquid <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> and a hard rigid outer shell (or <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>), which is composed of the Earth's rocky <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> and the deeper mostly solid outer layer of the mantle. On land the crust is known as the <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a> while under the sea it is known as the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_crust" title="Oceanic crust">oceanic crust</a>. The latter is composed of relatively dense <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> and is some five to ten kilometres (three to six miles) thick. The relatively thin lithosphere floats on the weaker and hotter mantle below and is fractured into a number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plates" title="Tectonic plates">tectonic plates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> In mid-ocean, magma is constantly being thrust through the seabed between adjoining plates to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-oceanic_ridge" title="Mid-oceanic ridge">mid-oceanic ridges</a> and here convection currents within the mantle tend to drive the two plates apart. Parallel to these ridges and nearer the coasts, one oceanic plate may slide beneath another oceanic plate in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a>. Deep <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">trenches</a> are formed here and the process is accompanied by friction as the plates grind together. The movement proceeds in jerks which cause earthquakes, heat is produced and <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> is forced up creating underwater mountains, some of which may form chains of volcanic islands near to deep trenches. Near some of the boundaries between the land and sea, the slightly denser oceanic plates slide beneath the continental plates and more subduction trenches are formed. As they grate together, the continental plates are deformed and buckle causing mountain building and seismic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Earth's deepest trench is the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a> which extends for about 2,500 kilometres (1,600 mi) across the seabed. It is near the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Islands" title="Mariana Islands">Mariana Islands</a>, a volcanic <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> in the West Pacific. Its deepest point is 10.994 kilometres (nearly 7 miles) below the surface of the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smmt_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-smmt-68">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coasts">Coasts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Coasts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Praia_da_Marinha_2017.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Praia_da_Marinha_2017.jpg/220px-Praia_da_Marinha_2017.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Praia_da_Marinha_2017.jpg/330px-Praia_da_Marinha_2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Praia_da_Marinha_2017.jpg/440px-Praia_da_Marinha_2017.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Praia_da_Marinha_2017.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Praia_da_Marinha" title="Praia da Marinha">Praia da Marinha</a> in <a href="/wiki/Algarve" title="Algarve">Algarve</a>, Portugal</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Finland_2018-07-07_(43651908284).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5603" data-file-width="4616" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Finland_2018-07-07_%2843651908284%29.jpg/170px-Finland_2018-07-07_%2843651908284%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Finland_2018-07-07_%2843651908284%29.jpg/255px-Finland_2018-07-07_%2843651908284%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Finland_2018-07-07_%2843651908284%29.jpg/340px-Finland_2018-07-07_%2843651908284%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Finland_2018-07-07_(43651908284).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> in the archipelago of <a href="/wiki/Turku" title="Turku">Turku</a>, Finland</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">Coast</a></div>
<p>The zone where land meets sea is known as the <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coast</a> and the part between the lowest spring tides and the upper limit reached by splashing waves is the <a href="/wiki/Shore" title="Shore">shore</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Beach" title="Beach">beach</a> is the accumulation of sand or <a href="/wiki/Shingle_beach" title="Shingle beach">shingle</a> on the shore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monkhouse291_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monkhouse291-69">[65]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Headland" title="Headland">headland</a> is a point of land jutting out into the sea and a larger <a href="/wiki/Promontory" title="Promontory">promontory</a> is known as a <a href="/wiki/Cape_(geography)" title="Cape (geography)">cape</a>. The indentation of a coastline, especially between two headlands, is a <a href="/wiki/Bay" title="Bay">bay</a>, a small bay with a narrow inlet is a <a href="/wiki/Cove" title="Cove">cove</a> and a large bay may be referred to as a <a href="/wiki/List_of_gulfs" title="List of gulfs">gulf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup> Coastlines are influenced by a number of factors including the strength of the waves arriving on the shore, the gradient of the land margin, the composition and hardness of the coastal rock, the inclination of the off-shore slope and the changes of the level of the land due to local uplift or submergence. Normally, waves roll towards the shore at the rate of six to eight per minute and these are known as constructive waves as they tend to move material up the beach and have little erosive effect. Storm waves arrive on shore in rapid succession and are known as destructive waves as the <a href="/wiki/Swash" title="Swash">swash</a> moves beach material seawards. Under their influence, the sand and shingle on the beach is ground together and abraded. Around high tide, the power of a storm wave impacting on the foot of a cliff has a shattering effect as air in cracks and crevices is compressed and then expands rapidly with release of pressure. At the same time, sand and pebbles have an erosive effect as they are thrown against the rocks. This tends to undercut the cliff, and normal <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> processes such as the action of frost follows, causing further destruction. Gradually, a wave-cut platform develops at the foot of the cliff and this has a protective effect, reducing further wave-erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monkhouse291_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monkhouse291-69">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Material worn from the margins of the land eventually ends up in the sea. Here it is subject to <a href="/wiki/Attrition_(erosion)" title="Attrition (erosion)">attrition</a> as currents flowing parallel to the coast scour out channels and transport sand and pebbles away from their place of origin. Sediment carried to the sea by rivers settles on the seabed causing <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">deltas</a> to form in estuaries. All these materials move back and forth under the influence of waves, tides and currents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monkhouse291_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Monkhouse291-69">[65]</a></sup> Dredging removes material and deepens channels but may have unexpected effects elsewhere on the coastline. Governments make efforts to prevent flooding of the land by the building of <a href="/wiki/Breakwater_(structure)" title="Breakwater (structure)">breakwaters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seawall" title="Seawall">seawalls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">dykes and levees</a> and other sea defences. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Barrier" title="Thames Barrier">Thames Barrier</a> is designed to protect London from a storm surge,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> while the failure of the dykes and levees around <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> during <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a> created a <a href="/wiki/Humanitarian_crisis" title="Humanitarian crisis">humanitarian crisis</a> in the United States.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_cycle">Water cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Water cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">Water cycle</a></div>
<p>The sea plays a part in the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water or hydrological cycle</a>, in which water <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporates</a> from the ocean, travels through the atmosphere as vapour, <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condenses</a>, falls as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">rain or snow</a>, thereby sustaining life on land, and largely returns to the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup> Even in the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Desert" title="Atacama Desert">Atacama Desert</a>, where little rain ever falls, dense clouds of fog known as the <a href="/wiki/Camanchaca" title="Camanchaca">camanchaca</a> blow in from the sea and support plant life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>In central Asia and other large land masses, there are <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">endorheic basins</a> which have no outlet to the sea, separated from the ocean by mountains or other natural geologic features that prevent the water draining away. The <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> is the largest one of these. Its main inflow is from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Volga" title="River Volga">River Volga</a>, there is no outflow and the evaporation of water makes it saline as dissolved minerals accumulate. The <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a> in Kazakhstan and Uzbekistan, and <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_Lake_(Nevada)" title="Pyramid Lake (Nevada)">Pyramid Lake</a> in the western United States are further examples of large, inland saline water-bodies without drainage. Some endorheic lakes are less salty, but all are sensitive to variations in the quality of the inflowing water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_cycle">Carbon cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Carbon cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_carbon_cycle" title="Oceanic carbon cycle">Oceanic carbon cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biological_pump" title="Biological pump">Biological pump</a></div>
<p>Oceans contain the greatest quantity of actively cycled carbon in the world and are second only to the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> in the amount of carbon they store.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlobalCarbonCycle_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalCarbonCycle-75">[71]</a></sup> The oceans' surface layer holds large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Dissolved_organic_carbon" title="Dissolved organic carbon">dissolved organic carbon</a> that is exchanged rapidly with the atmosphere. The deep layer's concentration of <a href="/wiki/Total_inorganic_carbon" title="Total inorganic carbon">dissolved inorganic carbon</a> is about 15 percent higher than that of the surface layer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarmiento_and_Gruber_2006_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sarmiento_and_Gruber_2006-76">[72]</a></sup> and it remains there for much longer periods of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prentice_etal_2001_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prentice_etal_2001-77">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">Thermohaline circulation</a> exchanges carbon between these two layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlobalCarbonCycle_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalCarbonCycle-75">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon enters the ocean as atmospheric carbon dioxide dissolves in the surface layers and is converted into <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>CO<sub>2</sub> <sub>(gas)</sub> ⇌ CO<sub>2</sub> <sub>(aq)</sub></dd>
<dd>CO<sub>2</sub> <sub>(aq)</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub></dd>
<dd>H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> ⇌ HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd>
<dd>HCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> ⇌ CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>It can also enter through rivers as dissolved organic carbon and is converted by photosynthetic organisms into organic carbon. This can either be exchanged throughout the food chain or precipitated into the deeper, more carbon rich layers as dead soft tissue or in shells and bones as <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>. It circulates in this layer for long periods of time before either being deposited as sediment or being returned to surface waters through thermohaline circulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prentice_etal_2001_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prentice_etal_2001-77">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_in_the_sea">Life in the sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Life in the sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/170px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/255px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/340px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">Coral reefs</a> are among the most <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiverse</a> habitats in the world.</div></div></div>

<ul>
<hr/>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Marine_coastal_ecosystem" title="Marine coastal ecosystem">Coastal habitats</a></b>
</p>
<li style="border-left: 24px solid powderblue; line-height: 22px; padding-left: 0.7em"><a href="/wiki/Littoral_zone" title="Littoral zone">Littoral zone</a></li>

<p><b>Open ocean</b>
